Table of Contents

Opening

This framework has held, from the start, one standing claim: to be interrogated is not optional. A philosophy, once it stands, stands under the interrogation of others — its validity is not proclaimed by its own house, but settled with interrogation as a participant. And interrogation leaves one freedom of sequence: before the other's interrogation arrives, one's own interrogation of oneself can come earlier.

This book is that earlier interrogation.

The examination it would owe before judging others, it has arranged for itself first: one axiom laid on the edge of the knife, to see whether it retreats; every verdict interrogated for how far it reaches; six postulates, each brought its strongest opponent; eight illusions called by name in the house's own ledger first; one formal language taught to declare every instrument it borrows; and at the last, the sixteen-layer road — the thing this framework treasures most — graded station by station, the heaviest cuts falling on its own favorite sentences.

This is not asceticism; it is arithmetic. Since interrogation comes sooner or later, to ask once early is to have the ledger cleaner once. Self-questioning cannot warrant the discovery of all one's blind spots, but it can register the visible debts first — the rest is left to you. From the moment this book is finished, it stands under interrogation; from the moment you open it, the one interrogating — is you.

Rules of the House

This book has set itself a few laws of writing, recorded here — not as self-description, but to arm the reader: only when the law is in the open does interrogation have a handle.

One. Two voices. External objects — Kant, other philosophies, the objects of cognition — are treated in an objective voice; the examination of this framework itself — dismantling, downgrading, retracting — in a first-person critical voice; commitments and retractions are signed "we."

Two. No backstage. The text does not explain its own style, announce its sections, or narrate its writing process; it does not report what it has done, not done, or made ready. Where limits must be stated, they are stated as jurisdiction.

Three. No self-praise. The book issues itself no certificates of virtue; it states structures, states costs, and keeps its commitments — virtue is the reader's verdict, not the author's claim.

Four. Metaphor carries no definitions. Every term is defined first in strict wording; metaphor may only annotate what has already been defined.

Five. Papers and chapters. Each chapter of this book stands as a paper in its own right; "this paper" in the text refers to the present chapter, and cross-references use the paper names.

Six. Registration and quotation. Every commitment is registered with its content, status, source of warrant, and alternative branches; every quotation from the corpus is checked against the original; revisions, translations, and readings of the corpus each have their own ledger — the revision queue, the fidelity registry, the reading registry — all published with this book.

If the reader catches this book violating its own law, that is precisely the use this law was made for.

Chapter One · Introduction

1 · What We Are Asking

Kant set the master question of the Critique of Pure Reason: how are synthetic judgments a priori possible? The weight of that question lies not in its wanting an answer, but in its demand that reason turn around — no longer using reason to know objects, but making reason look back and examine what reason itself can know, and where it oversteps. That is the original sense of Kritik: not blame, not rejection, but the drawing of a faculty's own boundary and warrant. On this Kant wrote three Critiques, each examining one capacity of reason: cognition, practice, judgment.

The First Critique of SAE (Self-as-an-End) inherits exactly this move — and none of Kant's conclusions. What it turns around to examine is not "reason" but the operation of negation. Its master question is therefore parallel to Kant's in form, and lands somewhere else in content.

— And here the edge of the examination turns toward ourselves.

What we ask is this: what are the formal invariants of negation as an operation? And: this core, used for every act of cognition — by what right would it be exempt from examination by its own rule?

The second question is the true entrance. We are about to claim that negation is the operative medium of every cognitive act — positing, distinguishing, inferring, doubting: none is done except through negation. But the moment we claim this, a reflexive question falls straight back on us: if everything must pass the examination of negation, then by what right are negation itself, and the whole apparatus of claims we have built around it, exceptions? To exempt one's own foundation from interrogation is to issue oneself a certificate one issues to no one else — and exemption is precisely what a critique exists to abolish.

So the answer can be given now, and it fixes the character of this critique: no exemption. The core of negation cannot be excused from self-examination, and neither can any claim built around it. The First Critique is not a separate work we perform outside this core; when the rule "everything passes the examination of negation" is carried through to the core itself, the act that then takes place is the First Critique. This system turns the edge of negation upon itself — that is the First Critique.

As for what the formal invariants of negation actually are: what has so far proved irrefutable is one, exactly one; the rest are commitments — postulates, undertaken and replaceable. This conclusion cannot be established by announcement. It can only be obtained as what remains after a dismantling: taking down several things that looked necessary and are not, including a few sentences we ourselves once spoke as foundations.

2 · Dismantling Three Things Mistaken for Necessities

Terms first. SAE takes negation as the basic operation of cognition: to know an object is to perform one demarcation upon it — to part "this" from "not-this." "Negativa" in this book names, first of all, this performable operation of demarcation; negation in propositional logic and rebuttal in speech are its instances within language, and it is not identical with the Boolean connective ¬. The relatively stable distinction that one demarcation deposits is called a construct; what one demarcation fails to exhaust — its primary form being the residue, on the demarcated side, that this cut did not take in — is called the remainder. One example: part "red" from "not-red," and "red" is a construct. Even if you give it a boundary precise to the wavelength, the construct still has not taken in brightness, saturation, viewing conditions, and each concrete appearing — these aspects, unexhausted by this demarcation, are its remainder. Remainder is not another name for vagueness. Identity, difference, space-time, causality are all constructs of this kind, each carrying its own remainder.

To demarcate again upon the remainder, gaining a new construct and leaving a new remainder: this repeatable operation is called chiseling. The sequence of constructs accumulated by chiseling, from the most formal identity up to self-awareness and meaning, is called the DD sequence (its layers written 1DD, 2DD, ...).[^dd]

Around this apparatus there was once a set of necessity-claims: the subject necessarily constructs; negation necessarily sediments into constructs; a construct necessarily leaves a remainder; the remainder necessarily forces the next cut — the sequence like a machine that turns by itself. We once said exactly this. Now take this set of "necessities," one by one, to the edge of negation, and see which are necessities at all.

First: at the level of cognition, the subject is not "compelled" to construct

We once wrote this machine as an inescapable forced cycle: the subject cannot but construct, cannot but be driven by the remainder, cannot but chisel the next cut. The claim does not survive one simple question: that "cannot but" — was it proved, or asserted?

Imagine a being that chisels one construct, faces the remainder that follows, and stops — chisels no more. Is there a contradiction in this thought? None. It can stop. Then "the subject cannot but go on chiseling" is not a necessity but a compulsion we quietly installed. So here the claim must be lowered: at the level of cognition, it is not that the subject cannot but chisel; the subject can chisel, and can stop. Chiseling is its active deed, not the necessary running of a machine.

But the dismantled "cannot but" must have its boundary drawn precisely, or the dismantling wounds SAE's own core. It is the epistemological "cannot but" — the assertion that this sequence of constructs necessarily unfolds, like a self-turning machine — and that cannot be proved (the sequence can stop). It is wholly distinct from another "cannot but": the subject cannot but legislate for itself and keep the law it has laid down — the practical "cannot but," inherited from the autonomy-form of Kant's categorical imperative, and precisely the inner drive of SAE.

And this practical "cannot but" must be understood exactly, or we repeat the very error just dismantled — smuggling "chiseling" in as a necessity. Its universality is formal only: every subject cannot but keep the law it has itself laid down (that is the form of autonomy); the content of the law differs from subject to subject. What law one lays down for oneself varies: for one subject the self-given law is exhaustive construction, chiseling without cease; for another, the self-given law is precisely not to construct — losing day by day, doing without doing, no chiseling. So the practical "cannot but" does not universally drive anyone to construct; it drives each to keep their own law, and that law may be construction, or its refusal. To equate this "cannot but" directly with "cannot but chisel" would be to smuggle construction in as universal necessity once more — this time from the practical side.

And this, precisely, is freedom rather than its loss: a subject keeping a law it laid down for itself is no servant of necessity but free — because the law that drives it is its own (heteronomy unfree, autonomy free — the equation is inherited from Kant; what is inherited is the problem of self-legislation, and what is rewritten is the condition under which a self-given law obtains its validity). Laozi keeping the law "do not construct," and a philosopher keeping the law "construct exhaustively," are equally free — in this book's usage, freedom lives in the form "keeping the law one has laid down oneself," not in any particular content of the law.

The Reason That Cannot-But — SAE's paper on the practical side — rewrites the four formulas from "ought" sentences into four "cannot-but" sentences, and autonomy is no longer listed as a parallel third item but becomes the ground color running through all of them. "Ground color" means exactly this: the form runs through every dimension, while the content of the law is laid down by each subject. This is an inheritance from Kant, and a rewriting of him — and where the rewriting lies must be recorded. In Kant, the form of universal law is not a test appended to self-legislation but a constitutive condition of autonomy: there is only one test, though many maxim-contents may pass it. Here, withdrawing that test's exclusive validity is a redefinition of autonomy, not a mere loosening of content: self-given laws of different content ("do not construct" is also a law) are not judged wrong, but registered as different branches, each with its conditions and its costs. And the cost must be stated exactly — overstated, it becomes another dogmatism: a subject who halts this operation no longer realizes its being-an-end through this operation — that is all; this cancels neither its normative standing as an end, nor licenses anyone to treat it merely as a means (the strict argument for this layer belongs to the practical critique).

Only: this practical "cannot but" belongs to the jurisdiction of the Second Critique — the critique of the self's practice — not here. This First Critique examines the method; here it marks: at the epistemological level, there is no necessary motor guaranteeing the sequence's unfolding.

Dismantle the epistemological "cannot but," and a more fundamental question is pulled out at once: if the remainder no longer "forces" the next cut, then at the level of cognition, by what does the next cut appear? In other words: this sequence — where is its motive power?

The answer is not in the remainder. The remainder is only a gap, a stretch left unexhausted; by itself it pushes nothing — to say "the remainder forces the next cut" is to speak of a static gap as if it were a force. The next cut appears because the subject, at that gap, performs negation once more. The power is in this "once more," not in the gap. And this "once more" is the subject's own initiative, dispensable — so the sequence has action, and no motor that guarantees its turning.

Nor is "undriven" the same as free — to equate the two is to slide back into the confusion just dismantled. Epistemic stoppability, motorlessness, is not itself freedom; it is freedom's room. Precisely because cognition does not compel construction, the subject can legislate for itself in practice — lay down the law "construct exhaustively," or the law "do not construct." Had epistemology locked the subject into necessary construction, the law "do not construct" could never be chosen, and a subject like Laozi could not exist. So the "can stop" here is not freedom itself; it clears the ground for practical freedom: real freedom is keeping the law one has laid down (that is the Second Critique's affair), and the absence of epistemic necessity guarantees that the law can genuinely be the subject's own choice, not locked in advance.

With this, an apparent contradiction dissolves: in cognition, construction can stop; in practice, a subject that takes itself as an end walks its self-given law without cease — even if its law is "do not construct." The two not only do not conflict; they condition each other: able to stop, and yet unceasing — that is autonomy; unable to stop, and unceasing — that is only a machine turning. This is the necessary shape of autonomy, not yet the whole of it — what is able to stop yet unceasing may also be obsession, and parting autonomy from obsession is the practical critique's work. Stoppability gives the room; the unceasing is the law the subject lays down and keeps within that room. This critique adjudicates the former; the latter is the Second Critique's topic. The two critiques meet here — and standing at the seam is freedom itself.

Second: Negativa is the sole axiom — but "axiom" does not mean "everything derives from it"

With compulsion dismantled, one deeper and more seductive claim remains: Negativa is the sole axiom, and everything else — construct, remainder, the entire DD sequence — its theorems. One unshakable root, and all things derived from it. The claim has an architectural solemnity, and for exactly that reason it must be watched — for what we have been dismantling all along is precisely this foundationalism of "one root bearing all."

The crux: the word "axiom" carries two things at once. One is status: the axiom as the first principle that cannot be refuted from outside and cannot but be accepted. The other is productivity: the axiom as the source from which everything else is derived. When we first said "Negativa is the sole axiom," we meant the former — and without noticing, let the latter pass as granted. That default was the foundationalist garment.

Take productivity first. One conceptual separation suffices to strip its warrant. Imagine a minimal system containing only negation: it flips any proposition into its opposite, and flips it back. In this system negation exists, cannot be denied, and can be applied again and again — yet it only oscillates between a proposition and its opposite: it can flip, but it cannot flip its way out — it generates no continually fresh remainder, no reflexivity, no forward-growing sequence. Satisfying "negation cannot be denied" does not suffice to produce these — remainder, reflexivity, an open sequence do not come automatically with Negativa; Negativa does not entail all things. This half is withdrawn.

But the status half stands, and must be kept. "Negativa is the sole axiom" — in the sense of an irrefutable first principle — is true. To test whether a claim deserves the name of axiom, there is an explicit criterion: can an intelligible and performatively consistent opposing position be formed? — where "intelligible" does not mean agreed with, but that such an opponent could really have existed in human history: a subject holding another philosophy, whose reasons can be understood. The opponent's "existence" is judged by the position: there will always be people who say the opposition; what counts is a position that can be performatively consistently held. Measure Negativa with this ruler. First state the axiom's content exactly: "the denial of Negativa cannot avoid enacting it" says that Negativa cannot be negated — the fact that there is negation cannot be denied; applying negation to Negativa itself is sayable in grammar and untenable in performance. It does not forbid the negation of a negation: a negated proposition may be negated again — ordinary object-level work. It blocks one thing only: negating Negativa itself. Can a performatively consistent position be formed that declares "there is no negation"? — No. The declaration is itself an act of negation: the deed instantiates what the content denies. One may negate any number of claims about Negativa; but on the single point that there is negation, every act of opposition testifies for it — a performative contradiction, into which any opponent falls on the spot. People who said "there is no negation" have really existed, and their words can be understood; what cannot really stand is a performatively consistent opposing position — the moment it is spoken, a demarcation has already been performed. A proposition that cannot be denied without enacting its content, this book calls a performative axiom. There is no performatively consistent opposing position; Negativa is therefore an axiom — so far, the only one.

"Only" is the result of a test, and the test has its jurisdiction: among the candidate foundational propositions registered and examined in this book, this one alone passes — every other either admits an intelligible opponent, or its warrant runs in a circle. The list is open: new candidates come, they pass the same test; whether other performative invariants — "difference exists," "there is an assertion now" — are only rewritings of Negativa is left to the formal work. Status is settled by the test.

Then what are the rest — that constructs sediment, that cognition is discrete, reflexivity, even the remainder? Since they fail that ruler (an intelligible opponent can always be imagined), they are not axioms but postulates we actively undertake upon Negativa. They are not theorems of Negativa (it does not entail them); they are things we ourselves set down, and are willing to answer for. For this status there is a ready paradigm: Euclid's fifth postulate. For two thousand years it was pursued as a theorem of the other axioms, without success; in the end it became clear it cannot be derived — because it marks a fork: replace it, and what you get is not error but another, equally consistent geometry. The structure here is the same: from Negativa these postulates do not follow — the minimal negation-only system is the conceptual separation's exhibit here; the item-by-item separation of the narrow formal renderings is given in Appendix II — and each one can be replaced. "Replaceable" takes an explicitly weak sense in this book: the claim enjoys no immunity from withdrawal or re-positing; it does not assert that a globally consistent alternative system has been proven to exist. The intelligible opponent points to the entrance of a branch; whether the branch is habitable must be separately built and examined — some branches have really been lived in (the pragmatist's, Laozi's), others so far can only be pointed at. Euclid's alternative branches were fully constructed and proven consistent — a difference of completion, and the reason the paradigm is a paradigm. ("Axiom" and "postulate" take these technical senses in this book, not the loose division of labor the two words have in modern logic.) But replaceability is not immunity from liability: on one's own ledger, when a postulate is pierced, the old version must die and be replaced by revision — replaceability does not exempt from death; it is precisely death's form. And here lies the parting from foundationalist postulates: foundationalism enshrines its postulates beyond replacement; here the postulates lie on the edge of the knife, replaceable at any time — and at any time possibly compelled to be replaced. They are undertakings, not foundations.

As for Negativa itself: with productivity withdrawn, its role is registered on two levels. The level the axiom warrants is narrow: in the act of opposing, Negativa cannot be bypassed — the performative contradiction proves this much and no more. The wider level — positing, inferring, perceiving, affirming: every cognitive act passes through Negativa's hands — is the operational postulate this book undertakes: SAE understands object-cognition as demarcation, and answers for the claim that every cognitive act it examines has Negativa as its medium. This postulate is replaceable, and its opponent is ready-made: a subject who understands perception as pure reception, containing no demarcation at all. Within this postulate, "without Negativa, not even a postulate could be posited" holds; but it says that the act of positing cannot do without Negativa — not that the content posited comes from Negativa: as speech cannot do without breath, though no sentence owes its meaning to breathing. Negativa is so far the only axiom, because it alone cannot be performatively consistently opposed; and it is the operative medium, because the undertaken postulate routes every examined cognitive act through it: at once the one starting point that cannot be bypassed in the act of opposing, and the tool that is always in use.

Third: the remainder cannot be eliminated — closest to the axiom, still a postulate

Postulates do not all weigh the same. One of them stands closer to the axiom than any other — close enough almost to join it, and in the end unable to. It is: the remainder cannot be eliminated.

The claim itself first: the remainder cannot be cleared away. For "clearing the remainder" would mean reaching a residue-free, complete cognition; and to cognize away even that last residue is itself one more cut — leaving a new remainder. The act of eliminating the remainder is precisely an act that produces remainder.

Now measure it with the ruler: can an intelligible subject be imagined who denies it? Yes. There is a kind of subject who says: no — the remainder does not exist at all, and even if it appears for a while, a sufficiently precise construct can eliminate it completely; cognition can be complete. This is no absurdity — it is a philosophical impulse with a long lineage, from Leibniz's mathesis universalis to certain ideal-language programs, all headed toward "cognition can be completed." We can understand such a subject. So by the test, "the remainder cannot be eliminated" does not, like the axiom, exclude every intelligible opponent — it is a postulate, not an axiom.

But it is an exceptionally stable postulate, closest to the axiom. For the opponent, though imaginable, has no firm footing: to claim he has reached completeness and eliminated the remainder, he must verify that "this cognition of mine truly misses nothing" — and the verification is itself one more act of cognition, one more cut, which may again bring out remainder. He does not fall into performative contradiction on the spot (hence not an axiom), but a shadow follows him always: he can never confirm his success, because the very announcement of success must be checked once more. Whoever opposes the axiom falls into performative contradiction immediately; whoever opposes remainder-indestructibility does not — but cannot, from inside his own verification, confirm success.

Why not then simply list it as an axiom? Two reasons. First: the only path to proving it irrefutable is "verifying completeness itself brings remainder" — but "verification (cognition) brings remainder" is itself an instance of remainder-indestructibility. To prove it by itself is a circle. Second: to install it as an axiom requires an extra presupposition — that all subjects stand on the level bound by this truth. We make no presupposition about subjects' levels: the absolutist who claims cognition can be completed is an intelligible opponent; his branch is registered, not cancelled; such opponents have really existed in history — it is therefore not a performative axiom. So it stays among the postulates.

Thus the claim closest to the axiom stays on the postulate side. Its content grows no lighter for that ("cognition can never exhaust itself; there is no Archimedean point — no standpoint from which cognition closes as a whole" remains one of the framework's heaviest assertions); what changes is only its position: it stands on the side that can be opposed, together with the other postulates. To enshrine it in an unquestionable place would turn the system, on the spot, back into the foundationalism it criticizes — that place cannot be granted to it. The cost is explicit: this heaviest of assertions can be replaced at any time.

Last, take apart a word we long used indiscriminately. What was loosely called "the conservation of the remainder" in fact contains two claims of different strength, which must be parted — and once parted, both are kept, only ranked at different places.

One is indestructibility, on the side of the subject's act: clearing cannot reach zero, because the act of clearing itself produces remainder. The other is conservation proper, on the side of the remainder's own persistence: a remainder does not evaporate of itself. A remainder "digested" by a later construct is transformed into a new construct and a new remainder, not annihilated; a question no one asks anymore is still on the ledger — being forgotten does not write it off. This conservation is not the quantity-conservation of physics — the remainder has no measure, so "total amount" does not apply — it is "transformable only, never annihilable": old accounts may be reorganized, may be merged into new ones, but cannot be struck out of nothing.

Conservation is a weaker postulate than indestructibility, and easier to replace. The ruler shows it: the opponent of indestructibility can never confirm his success; the opponent of conservation stands quite firm — he may fully grant that "clearing cannot reach zero" while holding that "a remainder no one asks after simply dies; a dead question is not preserved, it is dead." Such a thoroughgoing pragmatism is easy to imagine and entirely intelligible. So in the genealogy of postulates, conservation ranks below indestructibility, farther from the axiom.

Yet we undertake it, and the reason is explicit: the entire practice of this critique hangs on it. What we have done all along is record the remainders the examination turns up — which step was lowered, where something was withdrawn, what is still owed. If conservation failed — if unasked-after remainders could die of themselves — then forgetting would become one of the forms of solution; and we do not accept forgetting as solution. To undertake a more easily replaced postulate has its price, and the price speaks for itself. One thing only must be guarded: conservation is no motor. A remainder persisting on the ledger pushes no one to chisel at it; whether to chisel remains, always, the subject's affair.

After the dismantling: one axiom; everything else, undertaken

Three things taken down, and the "necessity everywhere" story has largely collapsed; at the root, the true shape shows. That the subject cannot but construct — lowered. That Negativa entails all things — withdrawn. That conservation and indestructibility were one claim — parted. And through the ruler of "can an intelligible opponent be imagined," what truly deserves the name of axiom — refutable by no one from outside — is, so far, one:

The denial of Negativa cannot avoid enacting it — the operative medium of negation cannot be denied, because every challenge to it must use it. It is, among candidates examined so far, the only first principle warranted by its own performance — and the tool always in use.

The remaining foundational claims are all postulates: undertakings we actively posit upon this single axiom, answer for, and may see replaced. The strongest is the remainder cannot be eliminated — closest to the axiom, still a postulate. After it, the conservation of the remainder (one grade weaker: the remainder transforms only, never annihilates; the ledger is not written off by forgetting). Further down, the operational undertaking (every examined cognitive act has Negativa as its medium), that chiseling sediments into constructs, that cognition is discrete, reflexivity... For these more ordinary postulates the alternative branches are easy to build, and they are the easiest to replace.

Someone will ask: with the foundation shrunk to a single thin axiom, can this framework still assert anything at all? The worry presupposes one thing: that a system's weight must rest on its unquestionable a prioris. The arrangement here is the opposite. The single axiom is indeed thin; it guarantees only that "there is an undeniable operative medium." The actual assertions — cognition can never exhaust itself; there is no Archimedean point; the human being is an end in itself — stand entirely on the postulate side, every one of them opposable by an intelligible subject. Nothing is asserted less; what is less is exemption: apart from that one performative axiom, no substantive assertion enjoys exemption from inspection. And the reverse arrangement is unworkable — enshrine the heaviest claims as unquestionable axioms, and the system turns back, on the spot, into the foundationalism it criticizes. So this thinness is not poverty of assertion; it is the shortness of the exemption list: one entry, because the test let exactly one through.

3 · With Kant: Opposite Directions of Travel, One Formal Meeting Point

Kant's critical enterprise has three parts: the Critique of Pure Reason examines cognition, the Critique of Practical Reason practice, the Critique of the Power of Judgment judgment — in each, reason turns around and draws the boundary of one of its own capacities. Running through all three is one common posture: what Kant asks is always the condition of validity — by what right cognition is valid, by what right the law binds, by what right a judgment may demand universal assent — never how these capacities came to be. On the theoretical side he asks by what right cognition attains objective validity; on the practical side, by what right the moral law binds a finite and imperfect will — both sides begin from formed capacities and ask after their validity, not their genesis. In this sense Kant's question is the question of right (quid juris): by what warrant — and only on the practical side does it strictly take the shape of an ought. Each part has its object: the first Critique faces the finite knower dependent on sensibility and understanding; the second, the finite and imperfect rational will; the kingdom of ends is the practical side's ideal — the systematic union of subjects who both legislate universally and obey the laws they legislate. As for the ideas of reason — God, the soul, the world-whole — Kant says explicitly that they are regulative, not constitutive: they describe no ready fact, but point the direction for an endless approach (the categories are not on this list: for experience the categories are constitutive — that is their theoretical use in the first Critique; on the practical side, soul and God receive instead the standing of postulates of practical reason, of which below). The imperative is an imperative precisely because it faces a will that is not perfect — for a holy will there is no "ought." Kant's critique is therefore retrospective: from capacities already formed, it establishes the a priori conditions of their claims' validity — he asks after validity, and writes no history of genesis.

SAE's First Critique is the first part of a plan likewise in three: a critique of the method — the operation of negation, and the whole apparatus of claims around it; then a critique of practice (how the subject legislates for itself), and a critique of judgment. The three are logically possible, not obligatory to complete; and the first is a precondition for the latter two, not their foundation — it goes first, yet remains itself under examination throughout; the latter two must accept that what they depend on can change. This map is formally parallel to Kant's three parts, but its source is different: it was not cut to Kant's table of contents; it differentiated itself within the sequence actually walked — only when the chiseling reaches the place where a subject can reflexively legislate does the critique of practice have an object to examine.

The relation of this sequence to Kant is development. We do not claim subsumption — subsumption would mean Kant's critique collected as one local practice on some stretch of the sequence, covered without exception by a larger frame. The very shape of that claim fails: one construct appointing itself the final framework, claiming to cover another construct without exception — when "Negativa is the sole axiom, all things its theorems" fell, what fell was exactly this posture of "one thing covering all"; the same posture with the subject changed — from Negativa to this framework itself — is no firmer for the change. Development, then — but the ledger of the development must be kept exactly. From Kant one thing only is inherited: the act of self-interrogation itself. At the root, the movement is inversion: the a priori, in Kant, is the argued condition of possible experience — validity only, no genesis; here it is sedimentation — genesis itself. The thing-in-itself, in Kant, is a fixed boundary; at the corresponding position in this book stands the remainder — what each demarcation leaves unexhausted; whether the two are one and the same boundary awaits the drawing of jurisdiction. Claims of universal necessity are given a priori grounds in Kant; here, whether each step of the sequence is necessary must be examined one step at a time. As for particular conclusions: supplementation, not overthrow. In one sentence: this is the continued unfolding of Kant's critical method under new conditions; and if one must say what it does to Kant, it is inverted development — called development, its core a reversal.

The direction reversed, the two roads' ends nonetheless meet in form. Set them side by side: Kant begins from formed capacities and traces back the a priori conditions of their validity; this sequence begins at genesis and chisels upward layer by layer, asking how these same structures form in operation. And at the far ends, the two roads partially meet in form — where, exactly, must be counted out. The framework's name — Self-as-an-End — answers Kant's second formula deliberately (The Reason That Cannot-But has rewritten the four formulas into four "cannot-but"s, autonomy as the ground color throughout); the two items erected on the sequence's later stretch — the subject legislates for itself, and answers "what is the human being" with "an end in itself" — are exactly the two determinations of a subject of the kingdom of ends; and the community of sequences — equal in standing, mutually examining, refusable and exitable — is one candidate operating form proposed for that kingdom: the kingdom is not presupposed; it is what ends actually make when they recognize and interrogate one another. Whether mutual recognition and mutual examination can grow into common legislation, rather than stopping at recognition, is left to the practical critique. Kant gave this form its normative statement within practical philosophy; this sequence arrives before it by the genetic road.

But the meeting point must be stated with measure — at two places. First, the convergence is formal only. The subject-shape at the end — "self-legislating end in itself" — is form; the content of the law does not converge: Laozi also arrives here in form — he self-legislates, and can stand in mutual recognition with other ends — yet his law is not to construct. And this end is the end of this sequence, the far point of the road that chisels "what is the human being," not the necessary destination of all subjective development; when we say "the final form as we hold it," that "as we hold it" is not an ornament but an index — erase it, and the formal convergence will be read as every subject's necessary destiny. Second, the guidance of the idea begins only at the layer where a subject can lift the idea. Kant writes no genesis-history of the rational capacities, so the idea's normative validity does not presuppose a subject's having completed a genetic sequence; starting from genesis, one must grant: before the subject bends back far enough to recognize that shape as its own end, nothing is guiding — every step of the sequence is still one contingent chisel-stroke after another. Guidance is not traction: the idea guides as a law the subject itself has laid down — able to stop, yet unceasing — not as a machine hauling backward from the end. Otherwise the motor just dismantled at the source would merely have moved to the sequence's far end.

The divergence thus narrows to a single axis — and not at the end, but on the road: the validity of that law along the way. Take the cognitive side first. Kant argued that human beings can know the world only through their own forms of sensibility and categories, and never touch the thing-in-itself — this marks the same kind of boundary as "the remainder cannot be eliminated": cognition cannot exhaust its object (whether the two are one and the same place of boundary awaits the drawing of jurisdiction). But same kind of boundary, two standings. Kant's argument addresses readers already capable of transcendental reflection, and its validity is taken to hold at once for all sensible-discursive knowers like us; to one who rejects the whole critical frame — a dogmatist confident of intuiting reality itself — Kant's verdict is: your claim is invalid. We do not make that presupposition: the absolutist who claims cognition can be completed is an intelligible opponent; we do not rule his claim invalid, but register it as another branch, labeled with its condition — he can never confirm success from within. The practical side is isomorphic: the non-conforming maxim is invalid in Kant; the different law is, here, a branch with its costs — validity indexed to the level the subject has reached and the law it has laid down. Gathered into one sentence: Kant's moral law is valid at once for every rational being; the law on this road is valid as far as it has been walked — the verdict of invalidity is replaced by the registration of branches.

Kant himself marked the seam. In the dialectic of practical reason, the immortality of the soul is introduced as a postulate, on the ground that the complete fit of will and moral law is holiness, which no rational being in the sensible world attains at any moment of its existence — hence an endless progress of approach is needed. The gap between ought and is, Kant himself ruled, closes at no finite moment. Translated back into this book's vocabulary, that is a practical remainder — entered on the ledger, not erased. But the comparison ends here, and the asymmetry must be spoken: Kant posits his postulates to make the endless approach itself possible — immortality underwrites the infinite progress of moral perfection, God underwrites the highest good's proportion of virtue and happiness; we keep the remainder precisely by refusing to register any approach as closure. So the two roads form a seam here, and the seam does not prove the two buildings were ever one: they meet at a crossing point — one tracing validity back to its conditions, the other tracking operation forward to its sediment.

4 · What Is the Human Being? — The Question Itself Was Chiseled Out

In the Lectures on Logic, Kant gathers philosophy into four questions: What can I know? What ought I to do? What may I hope? What is the human being? — metaphysics answers the first, morals the second, religion the third, and the fourth falls to anthropology; and then he adds a sentence of great weight: at bottom the first three all bear on the last, and everything may be reckoned anthropology. Three questions each found their home; for the one question they jointly point to, Kant wrote no critique — his late Anthropology from a Pragmatic Point of View is empirical, pragmatic observation, not the critical work that takes "the human being" as its object. That place stands empty.

On this sequence, the question needs no importing from outside — it is born in one reflexive stroke of the chisel. On the sequence's early stretch, negation's objects are external: identity, difference, space-time, causality — the world's skeleton sediments layer by layer. But negation can turn reflexive (this is one of the undertaken postulates, and does not come automatically): it can take the performer itself as object — tool and object coincide, and the chiseling hand chisels itself. In this genetic narrative, before the reflexive turn there is only the pre-reflexive performer, not yet an "I" constructed by itself as object; the self-legislator spoken of earlier belongs to the practical critique's level. It is this reflexive turn that sediments a special construct: the "I." And this construct is like every construct: the same cut that carves it out carves out its remainder. But other constructs' remainders merely lie unexhausted along the cut; the construct "I" is different — the one carved out is precisely the one that can look back. The reflexive "I" runs into its own unexhausted part; and when it performs negation once more upon that unexhausted stretch — a step like any chisel-stroke: active, and able not to happen — only then does that stretch cease to be mere residue and turn to face it as a question: what am I? The question is born here. It was not carried into the sequence by philosophers; it is the stretch left unexhausted on the cut when the performer first constructed itself as object. And the remainder cannot be eliminated, while conservation refuses to book forgetting as solution — so this question cannot be cleared away. It can be transformed, decomposed, rewritten, like every remainder; but ceasing to ask is not having answered, and forgetting writes nothing off: it stays on the ledger in altered shapes, and walks with the human being.

Since the question is a remainder, answering it — if one chooses to answer, and answers along this method — is to keep chiseling upon it. The sequence's later stretch is that chiseling: the subject legislates for itself and walks its law without cease; the subject recognizes the same shape in other subjects, and is recognized — mutual recognition raises "what am I" into "what are we"; and these mutually recognizing subjects belong, empirically, to the kind "human" — whereby the question is indexed as "what is the human being": a first-person question become anthropological through recognition and kind-registration. Stroke by stroke, at the far end of this road, the answer standing on the cut is: the human being is that which is an end in itself — self-as-an-end. The answer has a strange shape: the one who asked is the answer — but not returned unchanged. The answer to "what am I" is not a thing found outside the "I"; the answering process reconstructs the asker: the "I" that has chiseled itself to where it can legislate, recognize, and be interrogated is no longer the "I" that first left this remainder. The question is born of the subject's emergence; the answer stands by the subject's reconstruction — question and answer fall on one genealogy of the subject, not back onto one static construct. It is a loop, not a closure: the reconstructed "I" carries its own remainder in turn; the end is the end of this road, not the end of chiseling.

The standing of this answer measures cleanly with the ruler: postulate side. "The human being is an end in itself" — can intelligible opponents be imagined? Yes, and more than one kind: those who define the human as instrument, as pure process, or who refuse the category "end" altogether — each inhabiting its own geometry. So it is replaceable; it is the undertaking we hand over at the end of this road — a normative self-interpretation, not an empirical definition of the species — a theorem derived from nowhere. Its jurisdiction is likewise marked: what this road chisels is the human being; the answer is about the human, not about everything. The craft of chiseling can be carried to other objects — world, society, language — but each object must be chiseled anew, seeping its own remainder, growing its own sequence; the craft travels, the finished work does not: the figure carved in this material cannot be moved to another and declared to dwell there too.

What remains is this critique's proper task: to lay the road from the "I"'s remainder to "an end in itself" open, step by step, for examination — within the postulates and premises already laid, which step has only one exit, which chose one exit among many, and which is only sediment misrecorded as necessity. The rules of the examination are already set: one axiom, all else replaceable; the ledger is not written off by forgetting; stopping is possible, and continuing is the subject's affair. This critique examines the path of genesis and the standing of warrants; normative validity it does not adjudicate in advance. That the road reached an answer does not mean every step on it withstands interrogation — the work of critique only truly begins here.

[^dd]: "DD" continues the framework's early notation of dimensions as "D"; when the layers were subdivided, the letter was doubled, and the name is now fixed as detailed dimension.

Chapter Two · On Jurisdiction

1 · The IOUs, and the Question: How Far Does a Claim Reach?

Kant's critical philosophy was, from the start, an enterprise of boundary-drawing. The Critique of Pure Reason expressly rules the noumenon a limit-concept (Grenzbegriff): of negative use only, setting a bound to the presumption of sensibility, adding no object to cognition. The introduction to the Critique of the Power of Judgment then supplies a whole vocabulary of terrain: a concept has its field (Feld); the part of the field we can cognize is its territory (Boden); the part of the territory where the concept can legislate is its domain (Gebiet). The understanding legislates in the domain of the concepts of nature, reason in the domain of the concept of freedom; and a great part of critique is the work of preventing either from legislating across its border. Which claim is valid where — in Kant this question has a name, an institution, a border patrol.

At the close of the Introduction, three IOUs of this same kind stood on our ledger. First: the Introduction said that Kant's "the thing-in-itself cannot be known" and this book's "the remainder cannot be eliminated" mark the same kind of boundary — while whether they are the same place of boundary was held in suspense; and the reason for the suspension was honest: the word "boundary" had not yet been anatomized, so sameness of place could not even be adjudicated. Second: the Introduction gathered the divergence from Kant into one sentence — "valid as far as walked" — but walked by what measure? Object, layer, branch, undertaken postulates: which of these enter the index, and how, was never stated; an index-maxim without a theory of indexing is only a promissory note. Third: the Introduction declared that this road's answer is "about the human being, not about everything" — the jurisdiction was marked, but never derived: by what right does the craft travel while the finished work does not? Where is the line drawn, and by what?

The three IOUs have an order. Without anatomizing "boundary," sameness of place cannot be judged; without the index, "as far as walked" is an empty maxim; only when the index stands can the line of "about the human being" be drawn anywhere at all. This chapter honors them in that order of dependence, and at the end writes out two new task-sheets — the subject as what it can never finish chiseling of itself, and where an end gets its source — each marked with the critique to which it belongs.

By this chapter's own rule, its jurisdiction is declared first: it examines the constitution of boundaries and the manner in which validity is settled; it adjudicates the truth of no particular claim; and every line it draws stands itself on the postulate side — replaceable, and on the ledger from the moment it is drawn.

2 · Anatomy of the Boundary: the Reverse Side, the Remainder, and a Name

Begin by registering, item by item, what one demarcation yields and what it owes — and the key to the registration is to part two negations: not-this-construct, and not-"this." Parting "this" from "not-this," the distinction that sediments is a construct — settled in the Introduction. But the construct is not "this" itself: the construct is cognition's one intake of "this," and intake must deviate. (The word "must" is not taken for free here: it is the content of the postulate that the remainder cannot be eliminated, not a new exempt item — the completeness-theorist who claims intake can be deviation-free remains its intelligible opponent.) This deviation is the remainder; and the deviation belongs to this cut, not to either side of the line — it enters the ledger in four basic modes. First, the intake-deficit, inside the line: between our defined "red" and red itself there is necessarily no thoroughfare — even with the wavelength boundary exact, brightness, saturation, viewing conditions, and each concrete appearing remain outside the intake. Second, the misplacement-deficit, across the line and in both directions: what is "this" gets classed as not — a patch of red under strange light falls into "not-red"; what is not "this" gets taken in. Each misplacement of the line books on both sides at once. And the verdict of misplacement is retrospective: "what is really this" is never given as an independent truth-ruler, just as red itself is never given — misplacement is exposed by later re-demarcation, by repeatable conflicts under review, by the failure of operational aims: the same discipline as the after-the-fact naming of the thing-in-itself. Third, the double-hit: this cut's criterion strikes both sides on one and the same thing — one surface enters "red" under one condition and "not-red" under another, while this cut never fixed the condition; what it exposes is a condition-index the criterion left out, not a being-and-not-being under one condition. Fourth, out-of-field: every cut falls within a presupposed field — red versus not-red divides colors; toward what lies outside the field the criterion issues no verdict at all — a sound is not "red," and not the "not-red" this cut demarcated either (it is not a color at all); it is neither "this" nor the not-"this" of this cut.[^catuskoti] These four are the basic booking-modes registered by this chapter: not claimed exhaustive, and not mutually exclusive partitions of one field's logic — they are four forms of a verdict-act's indebtedness, and the list stays open under audit.

The position of the reverse side can now be said exactly. The "not-this" that this cut demarcates, this chapter calls its reverse side; the reverse side is not another construct positively made, yet it is negatively co-determined by this line — where the line lies fixes what "not-this" is — and so it shares all the line's deviations: the misplaced cross it, the double-hit sits astride it, the out-of-field is not even in its field. Remainder and reverse side remain, therefore, two different things — but the parting must be stated exactly: the reverse side is one side of the line; the remainder is this cut's whole ledger of debts — booked at all four places. To equate the remainder with the reverse side is to mis-book one verdict's deviation as the other half of the world; to book the remainder only inside the line is to miss three of the four accounts. Both errors must be guarded. Two further provisos. First, "both is and is not," "neither is nor is not" assert no true contradiction in the world: all four sentences speak of this cut's criterion striking doubly, or striking not at all, upon the thing — the debt belongs to the verdict-act, not to the object; the four sentences grammar can form are, here, all bookkeeping upon this one cut. Second, none of the four is vagueness: the intake-deficit remains where the boundary is exact; misplacement and double-hit occur where the criterion is strict — what erred is an unfixed condition, not a blurry line — and out-of-field is the field's own debt, the field itself being an earlier construct with its own remainder. The remainder is still not another name for vagueness.

The next stroke of the chisel has two places to land. Landing on the remainder is deepening: taking in what was left out — constructing brightness in as well — or correcting a misplacement, fixing a double-hit's condition — a finer construct; and the new construct is still an intake, still deviating: the remainder is transformed, not cleared. Landing on the reverse side is opening anew: raising a new "this" outside the old. The two directions lie on the two sides of the line — and every operation actually takes something: the new construct sediments; the old remainder transforms and merges into the new account. The taking is real.

The four modes do not stand equal, and this must be booked explicitly. Misplacement can be corrected — cut again, retrieve the strays; the double-hit can be resolved — fix the missing condition into the criterion; out-of-field can be relocated — widen the field, and a sound too can come under verdict. Of the four registered modes, the first three admit local treatment — and each treatment is itself a new demarcation, leaving its own intake-deficit; and the intake-deficit, at the least, cannot be removed by treatment of its own kind — correcting, condition-fixing, field-changing are all still intakes, still deviating. So the exact mechanism by which "the remainder cannot be eliminated" hangs on the master ledger is: the modes can shift; the ledger does not empty — debt can move from one mode to another, and the account is not cleared. What this chapter registers as ρ is precisely this intake-deficit.

Now the borrowed name can be set in place. Every "this" has a self that no construct takes in whole; however many strokes fall on it, the non-thoroughfare between construct and it never reaches zero — deviation is transformed, not destroyed. For this never-fully-taken-in self of each "this," this book borrows a name from Kant's family and calls it the thing-in-itself. The signboard goes up first: what is borrowed is the limiting office of the noumenon in its negative sense — adding no object to cognition, negative use only — and it is here object-indexed and processualized; this is this book's technical rewriting, not a restatement of Kant's term (in Kant, the thing-in-itself, the noumenon in the negative sense, and the transcendental object stand adjacent and are not simply identical; only the limiting office is inherited here). Three rules then fix the name's use. First, it is an after-the-fact naming: no red-itself is first given, against which constructs are measured — red itself is never given; it is the constructs, in use, that show their own unexhaustedness again and again, and "red itself" is the name for that which every construct falls short of. To treat it as a pre-given standard and ask "which part of it does this remainder correspond to" is to let the product give directions to the source; that step is not taken. Second, it is not one but one each: red itself, causality itself, the "I" itself — the thing-in-itself is plural with its objects, and each re-chiseling of a "this" rewrites the unexhaustedness it marks. There is no single noumenal boundary standing behind all appearances; even "one interface" is too whole, too singular. Third, it is a limit-concept — meeting Kant at the limiting office that adds no positively determinable object: negative use only, marking "this construct has not exhausted its this."

The parting falls elsewhere, and deeper than "wall versus no wall." Kant is not without progress: empirical cognition may deepen without end, the regulative use of the ideas is precisely what drives it forward, and the practical side has, besides, an endless approach underwritten by postulates of practical reason. But in Kant, the progress of empirical cognition does not rewrite the boundary — however much more appearance is known, the thing-in-itself comes not one inch nearer; all progress happens on the appearance side, and the boundary is indifferent to it. That is the exact sense of its inertness: not that no one walks, but that however far one walks, it has nothing to do with the wall. Here there is only one map. Every intake actually takes from the unexhausted of its "this" — sedimenting a construct, transforming remainder, growing the ledger — and every new construct shows a new unexhaustedness: the boundary is rewritten by each operation, and open to each. So the parting from Kant's thing-in-itself, in one sentence: inexhaustible, yet advanceable. And one incidental gain. In Kant there stands, besides, the problem of affection: read affection as the causal working of things-in-themselves, and difficulty follows — the interpreters' solutions differ to this day. Here the problem need not be entered: the deviation is not delivered from beyond the line; it is right there in what the act of intake itself produces.

This "advanceable" must take three provisos at once, lest it invite back what was just dismantled. First, advanceability is a capacity, not a duty: no remainder forces the next stroke, and no terminus pulls from ahead — advanceable and stoppable are two faces of one coin, and together they are that room of freedom. Second, the sentence "advancing has value" must be split and settled by the very rules this chapter is building. The descriptive half this chapter can assert: advance is gain, not illusion — and the exact content of the gain is: some registered debt is answered; a new, distinguishable, reviewable construct is actually generated and booked; its quality is settled by subsequent review, not underwritten by correspondence with "red itself" — the ruler of correspondence is never given. Erroneous constructs sediment too; booking is not passing review: the ledger keeps those two columns apart. That exhaustion is impossible makes nothing already-reviewed false; the reading "since it cannot be reached, why walk" errs by taking no-terminus for no-gain. The normative half this chapter does not adjudicate: whether one ought to advance is a matter of the subject's self-given law, and its strict warrant belongs to the practical critique — the subject who does not advance (Laozi stopped the chisel-and-construct) is registered as a branch, as ever. A claim barely out of the mouth is split in two by jurisdiction, each half sent to its own address. Third, advance here is not approach. Approach requires a measurable distance; distance requires both ends given — and red itself is never given: it is not a target standing in the distance that one may come nearer to; it is the name of the fact that every construct falls short of it. The deviation is not a shrinking mileage; it is the constitutive difference between a construct and its "this" — intake and taken-in are not of one kind, and no stacking of intakes ever becomes the "this" itself. Advance is therefore gain, not approach: what is taken is real; the unexhausted remains, in altered shape. Kant's endless approach has a destination — holiness — and must be underwritten by postulates to be possible; here not even the geometry of "approach" is used.

The question the Introduction suspended now has adjudicable content. Kant's "the thing-in-itself cannot be known" and this book's "the remainder cannot be eliminated" mark the same kind of boundary: cognition cannot exhaust its object. But "whether the same place" — under the two anatomies, the question cannot even be asked in common. Kant draws one uniform boundary of validity by the faculty of cognition — the same line for all knowers like us; asking "same place" presupposes that a boundary is such a uniform where. Here the boundary is not a where; it is a ledger settled per object: each "this" has its own unexhausted portion, rewritten with each operation. So the ruling: same kind, not same place — not because the two places differ, but because here there is no singular "place" at all: the noumenal is not one bound; it is each object's own never-fully-taken-in self. In Kant's family several items stand adjacent without simple identity: the thing-in-itself, the noumenon in the negative sense, the source of affection. This book inherits the limiting office alone, and registers a fourth item of its own: the unexhaustedness of each intake. For the uniform boundary of validity, no counterpart is set; the problem of affection is not taken over — deviation is born of intake itself; the unexhausted is the remainder, each "this"'s own, transformable — and the name of its inexhaustibility is the thing-in-itself, plural with its objects.

This anatomy is epistemological, not cosmological: its object is how demarcation produces and how boundaries are constituted; whence existence comes is not in its jurisdiction. The name "thing-in-itself" asserts no reality independent of cognition, supplying remainders on its own — "red itself" is not a thing this chapter has deposited in the world; it is a marker on this chapter's ledger reading "this construct, unexhausted." Nor is this a refutation of idealism; it is a suspension of the realism dispute — the epistemological analysis here does not of itself entail idealism: chisel any object — the physical world, society, language — and every intake brings out its own deviation, demarcates its own reverse side; an object's remainder comes from the intake of that object, not from the answer to "what is the human being." That the craft travels while the finished work does not here receives its mechanism: what travels is the anatomy "every construct deviates"; what does not travel is any construct's particular haul.

3 · The Index of Jurisdiction: How Far a Verdict Walks

The concept of jurisdiction needs no new coining — it was born in the fourth account of the last section. Every cut falls within a presupposed field; outside the field, the criterion issues no verdict; out-of-field is not error but no-verdict. To carry this from one cut to one claim is this section's entire work. A claim is the product of many demarcations, and each demarcation carries presuppositions: the field on which it classifies, and the equipment present when the verdict was issued — the constructs already chiseled at that layer, the laws in force, the postulates undertaken. A claim's jurisdiction is the reach of everything its verdict presupposes: where the presuppositions stand, validity is settled as usual; where they do not, this criterion settles nothing — the verdict lacks, at that address, the standing to apply. This is no announcement that the proposition has no truth-value there (the semantics of truth is another account, which this chapter does not open); it is the announcement that no one is entitled, there, to issue verdicts by this criterion. Overstepping the jurisdiction while issuing verdicts as usual does not make a verdict false — it makes it a verdict without standing.

This chapter first registers four kinds of presupposition; the index accordingly has, for now, four basic dimensions. First, object: a verdict inherits the "this" targeted by the demarcations that produced it. Verdicts about red do not judge sounds — the trivial case of out-of-field; verdicts about the human being do not judge the world — the serious case of the same rule. Second, layer: a verdict using constructs of some layer presupposes that layer has been chiseled out. Verdicts speaking of "law" and "autonomy" presuppose a subject arrived at the layer of legislating; before the reflexive turn, verdicts about the "I" have nowhere to land — not rejected: there is as yet no one to receive them. The "where" of "as far as walked" is settled, first of all, on this dimension. Third, branch: a law obtains validity first on its own branch. The law "do not construct" does not automatically convict the constructor; the constructor's law does not automatically fault Laozi — a law's validity does not migrate with the index of whoever issues the verdict. Nor, however, is it pre-judged to end at its branch of origin: a law that claims cross-branch validity must separately establish common objects, a shared field, and cross-branch warrant — whether it has the standing to claim so, and how that standing is obtained, is the practical critique's docket; this chapter only registers the question and pre-writes no answer. What can be done at this chapter's level is registration: entering each branch on the map with its law, its conditions, and its costs. The Introduction's "the verdict of invalidity is replaced by the registration of branches" can now be said exactly: a verdict without cross-branch warrant does not take cross-branch effect automatically — registration goes first, validity is examined separately; registration is this critique's act, and the final settlement of validity has its own address. Fourth, postulate: inferences hold under the postulates undertaken — only with the postulate-set frozen can a step be said to have a single exit; replace a postulate, and the verdicts standing on it lose their footing. Replaceability thus propagates upward: no verdict built on postulates is firmer than the postulates under its feet. These four are the basic dimensions this chapter registers, not claimed exhaustive — the genealogical position of the subject's constructs folds into the object index; how the layer-difference of judge and judged, and the context of conditions, fold into the four dimensions is registered at each use.

Walk one middle-sized claim through the settlement. "The construct of red does not exhaust red itself" — object: red, and the several intakes of red; layer: the layer of making constructs and reviewing them is open; the claim uses no "I"-vocabulary, no reflexive requirement; branch: those who undertake the demarcation-reading — a subject who understands cognition as pure reception settles nothing on this claim, and is registered as a neighboring branch; postulates: the operational postulate and remainder-indestructibility are in place; withdraw either, and the claim loses its footing. By the four dimensions currently registered, the claim's minimal jurisdiction address is written; there it settles; beyond that address, its standing to apply must be examined anew.

One thing crosses all four dimensions — among candidates examined so far, exactly one. The performative axiom's warrant hangs on no field, layer, branch, or postulate; it hangs on the act of denial itself: at any address of verdict-issuing, the denial that tries to deny Negativa is still performing it. It is thus a cross-index constraint on the act of denying — the zero point of the index. The wider sentence — all speech demarcates — is the content of the operational postulate: indexed, not at the zero point. Outside the zero point, everything is indexed — the last section's anatomy is indexed (it stands on the operational postulate), and this section's theory of indexing is indexed too (it is a construct, with its own four debts, on the ledger). Cross-branch mutual examination thereby has its footing: the shared zero point, the common field, and auditing the other by the other's own ledger — examination crosses the line; verdicts without cross-branch warrant do not cross automatically.

The promissory note can now be honored. The exact reading of "valid as far as walked" is: a claim settles its validity where its presuppositions stand; where the subject has not walked, the verdict is not a failure but pending — the field is not open, nothing is judged. Conversely, overstepping has its first definition: carrying a criterion out of its field and issuing verdicts as usual. The presumption Kant patrolled at the borders of the faculties is booked here as an indexed verdict's field-transgression; which illusions overstepping systematically breeds, and how they sustain themselves, is the pathology that belongs to the chapter on illusion — here only the docket is opened.

The third IOU is paid with it. "About the human being, not about everything" is no gesture of modesty; it is the index's direct reading: what this critique examines is the sequence that chisels "what is the human being"; all its gains carry that object index, and the line is drawn where the index stops — drawn by the index, not by extra proclamation. The formalization of craft-travel closes here too, with one stake against self-exemption: the traveling craft itself stands on the postulate side — what the operational postulate registers is precisely "the cognitive acts it examines" — so the craft arrives everywhere as an undertaking, never as a necessity; it is open to every object and exempt from examination on none. Were the craft to appoint itself necessary everywhere, the theory of indexing would fail on its own body.

Last, this critique's own address, written in this section's format. Object: the direct examinee is the operation of negation and the claims of the DD sequence; the object index of the examined sequence is the human being — the sequence that chiseled out "what is the human being." Layer: the path of genesis and the standing of warrants — how each step was walked, and on what it stands. Branch: this book's own — it audits its own ledger; other branches are registered, with equal standing to be registered, the question of validity going each to its own address. Postulates: all registered, all replaceable. Beyond this address — whether normative validity ought to hold, which branch is better, whence existence comes — each has its own door, and this chapter collects mail for none of them.

4 · Two Task-Sheets: the Subject's Noumenon, and the Source of Ends

With anatomy and index erected, two questions stand exactly on this chapter's boundary line — both pointed out by this chapter's own apparatus, both insoluble within it. On the object-list of things-in-themselves, one member is unlike the rest: the "I" itself. And the branch dimension of the index handles "law" and "end" at every turn, while by what right an end is an end has no source on the ledger. What this chapter can do for them is write the definitions clean, book what is already settled, raise the no-entry signs, and write the addresses — that is, task-sheets.

The first: the subject as its own noumenon. The definition follows from above: the subject's noumenon is the performer's never-fully-taken-in self — the "I" itself is an ordinary member of the object-plural list of things-in-themselves; what makes it extraordinary is that tool and object here coincide. The hand that paints red is not in the red; the hand that paints itself is the act of painting — and the act is not in its product. Hence, within the undertaken postulates, this step has a single exit: every construct of the self falls short, at least, of its own making — said exactly: the product is not identical with the performing event that produced it, nor can it enclose its own completion; it may represent the rules of its generation; what it cannot take in is the that-once of generating. The next self-portrait can take in the last act of painting; but the taking-in is itself an act, again not in the new picture. That is the first fold, on the side of the act.

The second deviation lies on the object side: the subject is moving. So long as the performer still performs, its state and its genealogical position are changing — each stroke sediments a new construct, transforms old accounts; where the chiseler stands is no longer where it stood before the stroke; the Introduction's close already registered the reflexive case: answering reconstructs the asker, and the "I" that finished answering is not the "I" that asked. So any intake of a subject takes the subject of that time: past cognition does not equal present cognition — not because the past took wrongly, but because the taken-in has walked on. Nor is this fold confined to self-knowledge; knowing any subject is like this: the other, too, is moving — your construct of him yesterday is a construct of yesterday's him. The subject's peculiarity is not "that it changes" (whether objects change is each object's empirical affair) but that the change is constitutive: every act of the subject alters its own state and standing. In the index's terms, the object index of a subject-construct therefore contains a position — it is an intake of one station on the genealogy; the old construct is not written off by time's passing — it stays on the old account, an intake of that station, its quality of that time still settled by that station's review; what is voided is treating it, without re-examination, as a verdict about today's station: that is overstepping, and "yesterday's construct impersonating today's performer" adds one more case to overstepping's pathology.

Red's remainder follows red because intake deviates; the "I"'s remainder follows the "I" for one reason more — every self-taking adds new untaken, and the taken walks ever ahead — two folds interlaced, which is why "what am I" clings closer than any object's question. The four accounts each have their shape upon the self: the intake-deficit — the self-portrait forever short of the painter; the misplacement-deficit — taking one's own doing for foreign, or the foreign for one's own; the double-hit and the out-of-field — those things about the performer that are both self and beyond self, or that slip the self/non-self division altogether — what they are called and how they are examined is precisely what is to be handed over. Kant paused at this same position: the Paralogisms rule that the "I think" accompanying all representations yields no substantial knowledge of a soul — what he booked there was an illusion; what is booked here is a remainder still at work. The addresses are three: how the reflexive construct sediments — to the audit of genesis; the several illusions of self-knowledge in which a construct impersonates the performer — to the pathology of overstepping; the normative subject that can legislate for itself — to the practical critique. What this chapter fixes is the definition, and the conditional necessity of that single step.

The second sheet: the source of ends. At the end of the road, the Introduction handed over "the human being is an end in itself," and declared at once: a normative self-interpretation, whose validity this critique does not adjudicate in advance. Yet the index handles "law" and "end" everywhere — branches are defined by laws, costs are settled against ends — and by what right an end obtains the standing of an end has never been entered on the ledger. Candidate sources can be listed: the subject's self-legislation? the mutual recognition of ends? even the reflexive structure itself — does taking oneself as object already make oneself one's end? Listing is where it stops; trying them one by one is not inside this chapter's door. Only one no-entry sign must be raised now: to infer "the subject is its own end" from "the subject chiseled itself out" is to carry a genetic verdict into the normative field and issue it as usual — overstepping; if anyone walks this shortcut, the pathology of overstepping has a case ready to open. One address: the practical critique. What this chapter fixes is the question's address, and this sign.

This chapter has here walked to its own boundary. Every line it drew was booked as drawn — the four debts all present, the postulates replaceable, validity ending here. The work beyond the line has its own addresses: the formalization of the constraints; the layer-by-layer audit of genesis; the pathology of overstepping; the critique of law and ends. As far as the verdict walks, so far runs the ledger; and the next step of critique begins from each address in turn.

[^catuskoti]: The four-mode booking pattern is inherited from Via Negativa (Methodology 0); traced further back, it is the four-cornered scheme of Madhyamaka (catuṣkoṭi: is / is not / both / neither) — the scheme predates Nāgārjuna, who worked it into a system and ended by relinquishing all four corners. This chapter inherits the four-part scheme only, refitting it as bookkeeping for one demarcation's deviations: here all four sentences are act-level accounts, none asserted as a truth of the world, and none used to certify the four modes' exhaustiveness. The borrowing takes the scheme's form; elsewhere the framework interfaces with Madhyamaka as an equal-standing dialogical partner — a separate matter. The various readings of the tetralemma within Madhyamaka's own field are outside this chapter's jurisdiction.

Chapter Three · On Illusion

1 · The Case Files, and the Question: False Claiming

Kant gave the second half of the Critique of Pure Reason to a doctrine of illusion. What the Transcendental Dialectic treats is not logical illusion born of momentary carelessness — that kind can be corrected, and once corrected it disappears; it treats transcendental illusion: reason naturally and unavoidably takes subjective principles for objective ones, and the illusion does not dissolve upon being seen through — Kant's analogy is the rising moon: after the astronomer has explained everything, it still looks larger. The Paralogisms, the Antinomies, the Ideal of pure reason are the three cases he opened. What critique can do against illusion is not abolish it, but fortify: let it go on appearing, while it no longer obtains validity unexamined.

The two preceding chapters have been handing case files to this one all along. In the Introduction we dismantled three things mistaken for necessities, and each thing taken down had the same shape: the non-necessary had entered the ledger under necessity's name.
